RATING: 8
Yet another worker placement game, but this one works well as it has a very well-integrated theme, and several modular expansions that improve overall gameplay.

The goal of the game is to score the most points before the game ends, and there are several ways to do this: 1) the main way is to complete portions of the Cistine chapel, 2) some of the portait cards score small points, 3) every 2$ scores a point at game end, 4) fulfulling the bishop's requests will score one time points and award paint every round at the cost of income earned, 5) completing stained glass windows, 6) gaining the favour of the bishop scores additional points when painting.  The game ends when there are 6 or less portions to be painted and/or there are only 2 portraits to complete (it ends in this round).

There are some somewhat original decisions to make, like what time to wake up (wake up early, get the best paints but pay a premium), like whether or not you want happy workers at the expense of generally missing out on turn order and "wasting" an action at the theatre, and whether to jump ahead or stay behind in scoring for turn order (similar to the scoring mechanic in Torres).  Each round you plan where your workers will go (secretly) after turn order has been established; you can go to market to purchase paints, the cathedral to paint the cathedral, complete stained glass windows to score points, 3) you can paint side job portaits for money and/or points and special abilities, 4) you can mix paint to create more varied and valuable colours, 5) go to the theatre to improve the mood of your workers and pontentially gain a bonus worker.

There are 3 built-in expansions that come with the game, as well as the Glaziers expansion, which includes 3 other expanions.  I can't imagine playing this game without the expansions, it would be far too fluffy.  But with the expansions it becomes an accessible gamer's game.  The scalability from 2-4 players is quite good, except you have to play with a dummy player in 2player, but the dummy actually works fairly well.  Not sure this should have been the DSP winner, but it's a very solid design, despite the fact that it's yet another worker placement game.  Very impressive for a first design for these designers as well.

Own the [boardgame=85925]Glaziers[/boardgame], [boardgame=86048]Scrolls[/boardgame], [boardgame=109140]Bishop's favour[/boardgame], and [boardgame=148289]Bells[/boardgame] expansions.

RATING: 3
110531 Its pretty, but I think worker placement games stress me out our something.  I'm not sure its the fault of the game but I'd rather play Agricola or PR or try a newer worker placement type game than play this again.

I think what I've realized is that I'm just not fond of simultaneous worker selection that has consequences.  In RFtG you still get your bonus (its a matter of letting other people do or not do actions), but I don't like that extra layer of "punishment" if you misplay your actions.   For some reason I find it very similar to Dungeon lords in that way.

Could well be a 4 if I played it more and if I wasn't so fond of the theme/artwork.

RATING: 6
[I]tl;dr - Fresco is a lightweight action-selection game that's solid, but ultimately forgettable.[/I]
[COLOR=#CCCCFF]____________________________[/COLOR]

I'd heard that Fresco hits the same high notes that [thing96913][/thing] did, so I picked it up in hopes of finding another winner from Queen Games. I found a good game (it's definitely a cut above mediocre garbage such as  [thing=219359][/thing] and [thing=182189][/thing]), but not one good enough that I wanted to own, or even really play again. 

I enjoyed the turn order track and the action selection -- it was interesting. Similarly, the different modules look good. But the base game, as it was, was far too simple and the decisions seemed obvious many times. 

Probably, the original Fresco was intended to be played with all three of the 'expansion' modules found in the base box, and the publisher stripped these out in an attempt to make a family-weight game. We did play with the brown and pink paints, but skipped the other two additions. 

Adding those in could make a difference, but I ultimately [i]did[/i] want a nice family-weight game, and base Fresco is not that experience. Perhaps my expectations were off -- it's entirely likely that Fresco with expansions is quite comparable to something like Lancaster, but in the end, my first experience of it was not enough to convince me to keep it.

Might use this as an introduction to worker placement games.

RATING: 8.84
It is a "classical" worker placement game with attributes I was looking for: not too long (like Anachrony, Agricola), easy to teach (a gateway game), can be a deep game (by adding modules). The theme is nice and family-friendly. The most interesting mechanic is placing your workers simultaneous in secret (somehow similar to Dungeon Petz) on your personal game board. Moreover, there is no blocking of workers which I really like: you decide at which time all of your workers will do their actions (early in the morning or late in the evening) which defines the initiative (first come, first serve).

Players: 2-4
Time: 60m

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
FAVOURITE GAME - it is a classic worker placement game, where you have to finish the fresco of a cathedral. To do so, you will have to decide what time your workers wake up for the day. The earlier you wake up, the earlier you will be in turn order, and the better options you will be guaranteed to have. Then you send your workers to buy dye, to paint portrait (for money), to mix colours, and to paint a portion of the fresco, to satisfy the bishop (which can award you extra points of victory). The box contains so called modules to make the game harder and suitable for more experienced players, otherwise it is a perfect family game.

RATING: 7
1st play: A very visually impressive game.  The mechanics are straightforward and easy to grasp, while feeling like a race game.  There are elements in here that remind me of Scoville, and I like that game but feel like it's a little too long sometimes to play with the family.  The built in expansions add just enough to make the game interesting and the hidden workers with the reveal make timing very important.

2nd play: Played this again and it was well received.  Everyone seems to like it and it is one step simpler than Scoville.  I really like how easy it is to explain and very accessible to artsy gateway gamers.

RATING: 6.8
One of those role selection euros that has a 'pick a wave' feel to it, in that it feels as though you're in essence trying to be out of phase with most other players to clean up on cheap resources (buying paints when they aren't, in particular).  On the whole, I'm not very fond of games in which the best path to victory is to do that little dance, but this one isn't too bad.

RATING: 8
[b]After 1 play:[/b]
A worker placement game, DRIPPING WITH THEME! Everything you do has a sense, theme-wise: you decide when to wake your workers (too early, you might lose one; later, you might receive one); you buy colors (cubes), you mix primary colors to get other colors, you use colors to paint the fresco, you pay the bishop to come and see your work closely; you paint other paintings to receive benefits or money; you send your workers to the theater to relax......WOW!
I love it and I can't wait to get more plays!
...it might have a problem with a runaway leader and not being to tight...

RATING: 8
Pros
- The theme of painting fits the worker placement game and cubes, unlike many others
- Colorful cubes and board
- Wake up mechanic and first dibs is fun to play back and forth along with mood of your workers
- Interesting decisions
- A good play time (1 hour and change)
- Add all the expansions in for fun, or don't. It's customizable that way. 
- Accessible to non-gamers and still requires long term strategy 
- An insert that has art on it. Pretty cool.

Cons
- You don't have to finish the fresco to trigger end game, which is weird for a game about painting a fresco

RATING: 8.7
For some reason, I love the theme of painting (not painting, literally, but a painting-oriented board game like this one, where you are acquiring paint, etc.), and needing to mix paints to complete certain parts of a painting.  I love the simplicity of the game, how it plays in a relatively short time period and how it's well-balanced insofar as you will need to visit all areas of the board.  I love the opposition of happiness with waking up early (I can relate!), and the constant dilemma that needing to choose one presents.  I can't really think of much I don't like, but I need to play the game more...

Update: after one more play, I find it a lightweigh euro with quite little to offer for experienced players. Can be 'safely' played once or twice, afterwards it is boring

RATING: 6
A solid, enjoyable Euro but I'm not this games intended audience. A couple of reasons why...

1. Caylus perfected Euros.

2. The game has a retard the players doing well, help the stragglers mechanism. I can ride a bike I don't need stabilisers.

3. With the mechanism above in play, you are pushed towards memorising hidden but trackable information to excel. To excel in Snap you could card count, is it worth the effort?

4. Queen Games, you insulted me by calling parts of the base game 'expansions'.

5. Stay up late and switch on a shopping channel. You'll see some ripped strong men and women trying to sell you some gimmicky home gym equipment, a shaky stick, electronic ab pads, a vibrating step... Fresco. Do yourself a favour put that TV remote down and get yourself to the gym... Caylus.

6. My buying Euro's days are over, abstracts are where its at.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Deceptive family game in that while it may have some very simple rules, the gameplay is packed with a lot of subtle strategy.  I can't suggest this to new gamers as other worker placement titles like Stone Age are for more accessible, but it is a great choice for people looking for a step up from gateway games. And the theme is pretty well realized in this making the rules easier to comprehend.  Overall a fantastic package which can be made as simple or complex as one desires. RATING: 9
I wonder what got us interested in trying this one? If you guessed Richard Ham you are correct! Curse that man and his love of Eurogaming. Were it not for him we wouldn't have half the games  we do. LOL. But he's backed a winner with Fresco. There is a lot of set up to it but at least you have something very pretty to look at once you're done. At its heart Fresco is worker placement and resource management at its simplest. We love the way it all coalesces into painting that beautiful fresco to score the points you need. There are just 2 things keeping this from a 10. 1) All the expansions. You really don't need them. They truly just add complexity for complexity's sake. Mind you we are saying this from a non-die-hard Eurogamer POV. So take that for what you will. 2) The Leonardo dummy player that is used in the 2-player game. We have no problem with dummy players in some games (Mage KNight and Pergamon) when they move independently. But a dummy player whose actions are controlled by the players is not a dummy player. No matter what you call it the dummy is a part of your strategy and it can be  a part of that strategy you plan for. Luckily we have devised a way for Leonardo to move on his own. Yes. It is random and involves dice so we can hear the die-hard Euros screaming once more but we prefer it to the designer's way because it actually makes him a "dummy" player not a part of the players' strategy.

RATING: 7.5
Painting a ceiling by acquiring paint from a market, mixing it and then going painting. When you choose to "get up" dictates what you pay for paint, but also what paint might be available as the opponents up earlier get to take paint first. It also dictates who paints a section first and you may find that what you were going to paint is already claimed.
Has an interesting turn order mechanism, where when you would score points to tie with an opponent you actually place on the space ahead or behind. Whoever is behind on points gets to choose when they get up the next round.

RATING: 8
Un precioso y divertido juego. El tema es conseguir pintar la mayor parte de un fresco de Iglesia. Y para hacerlo necesitamos que nuestra cuadrilla de ayudantes nos consigan los colores necesarios en el mercado y conseguir dinero (para poder pagarlos) a través de algún retrato a particulares. Si el ambiente que reina entre nuestra cuadrilla es bueno podemos aumentar el número de ayudantes, de la misma manera que si tenemos a nuestros ayudantes enfadados puede ser que perdamos alguno...

RATING: N/A
w/Glaziers expansion, and the Scrolls.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Worker placement is one mechanism that I get the least excited about.  It seems that these games often lead to massive bouts of analysis-paralysis, and don't offer enough fun to equal the time you will invest in playing.  But Fresco breaks that mold.  By forcing players to place their workers without knowledge of where others will go, decisions are made considerably faster.  The theme is also remarkably well thought out.  Every one of the game mechanisms makes sense because people just get the idea of keeping your workers happy, mixing your paints and then painting.

I haven't attempted any of the expansions yet, but I can see how they will be easy to integrate into future games.  What I like about the basic game, though, is that it keeps the mechanisms nice and straightforward.  This will make the game very easy to teach to new players.  Then, as they become acclimated to the basic game, you can add expansions as quickly as you think the players will be able to adjust to them.  This kind of thing usually isn't my favorite (asking the players to decide how they want the game to play) but for some reason, in this case I like it.  I will try to add additional comments when I try the expansions.

Thank You to my 2010 BGG Secret Santa!!

RATING: 7.5
Cada jugador lleva a un pintor y cinco ayudantes. El turno se divide en varias fases: primero se madruga, lo que equivale a situarse en una tabla horaria, que determina cómo afecta al estado de ánimo de los trabajadores y el precio al que puedes comprar colores en el mercado. Tras esa fase, cada jugador debe planificar de forma oculta el resto del turno... asignando acciones a tus trabajadores. Después se va al mercado y se compra al precio estipulado. Luego viene la colaboración en el fresco de la catedral. El estudio te permite conseguir dinero. El taller permite obtener colores compuestos,mezclando colores primarios. Por ultimo el teatro eleva el ánimo de los trabajadores y puede reportarte un trabajador extra para el turno siguiente.
El juego termina cuando finaliza la ronda en la que sólo quedan 6 losetas del fresco por dibujar. Es un juego de gestión de recursos sencillo y familiar, pero requiere planificación que aporta profundidad al juego. Recomendable. Solo lo he probado a dos jugadores (en cuyo caso hay un jugador ficticio). Intuyo que lo suyo es jugar al menos tres.

RATING: 9
Rating feels solid, following a lively first session competing for those premier victory point restorations on the fresco. After deciding turn order with the get up time, the action order changes. This has an important impact on the turn for getting the necessary paint purchases, money that will be spent, and who will get the best restorations (points) and possibly portrait advantages. Quite fun decision making worker placement game with no bidding or dice rolling. Seems to appeal to many different strokes of gamer types. Very well balanced game with alternating turn order. Excellent. UPDATE: Added expansion 3 with 1 (Portraits) and felt it helped spread out the scoring with the higher tiles. New paints add a little time, needed for calculating blending extra paints. UPDATE: Played all three expansions, adding 2nd expansion, Bishop's Request, making this more of a gamers game. Must consider with this expansion: what tiles to restore to get bishop tiles; value of bishop tiles received in the exchange; and paint rewards of bishop tiles. Rated medium for all three base expansions, and a 9 with expansions 2 and 3. UPDATE: Expansions 4,5,6 (Glaziers) raise this to a 10 (I don't own the Scrolls exp.). A work of art!

RATING: 7
Based on 1 play with 5 players:

Pretty fun! It's been awhile since I tried a good light Euro. Not a lot new here, but everything is simple and smooth.

The fact that the person in last place always chooses first makes for an interesting rubber band mechanic... and it's a small thing, but I also really like that when you land on the same square on the VP track as someone you can choose to go higher or lower... really makes that work just a little bit better, I think.

The wake-up-time vs. apprentice-happiness mechanic is also fun... it's one of those rare things that's both strategically interesting AND thematically amusing.

Looking forward to more plays!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.3

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is an excellent light eurogame! It's a game I like to introduce to people who have just played Stone Age as it's about the same difficulty. It's an entirely different game than SA and PotE and other worker placement games, and I think it is a game that fans of lighter WP games should own. Also, I need to say that it's nothing at all like Pastiche (which I also think is a great game) which some people claim. Sure, there are colors in both games and you need to get most victory points in the end, but that's it...

The mechanics aren't ordinary worker placement in the sense that everybody can go to every spot, but you do it in turn order, so you're not sure if the stuff you need will be gone (unless you're first player). And you're not sure if it's gone because you don't know if other players will go there because, at the start of the round, each player secretly decides where to go - so a big part of the game isn't really worker placement but rather simultaneous action selection. That's a pretty cool mechanic I think, though it can screw you over if somebody else takes what you need - but I guess you could just have planned for that! The mechanic about turn order is brilliant: wake up time! If you wake up early, you will be first at everything, but your meeples will be unhappy and you have to pay more... It's a great balance.

The box includes 3 "expansions", but I really think you need to play with all of them as it's just more fun and adds more decisions to be made; two more colors to mix and get more points from, portraits you can get other stuff than money from and finally you can do one other thing when you're mixing paints: merge 3 fresco tiles you've restored earlier in order to get some points and also a free paint each turn. 

There are also a bunch of other expansions for this game that you can mix and match however you like, so I think the replayability is good. The added complexety means that more experienced gamers will also find this game fun and challenging; however, there ARE better eurogames out there. I bought Fresco when I started playing board games, and it was great, but since then I've played better and more challenging/brain burning eurogames which I would rather play.

RATING: 7
Initial rating an 8. Fresco does a lot of things right. The theme is fun and matches the mechanics very well. Turn order matters, and it's cleverly balanced with a penalty for "early morning" work, and also more expensive prices in the market. The game is tight, it's probably a bit heavy for a straight-up "family game," but I'm impressed.

A random thought--this is not a worker placement game, as the way you delegate your "assistants" does not block other players.

Another random thought -- this blows Stone Age out of the water. Two attractive games of similar weight, but where Stone Age is uninteresting and bland point grabbing, Fresco forces interesting choices in each phase of a round. In Stone Age, you can build a hut of pure gold (???). In Fresco, you get to mix paints, choose your wakeup time, and all other things that make so much more thematic sense.

Update: Traded this away, but I still like it. I just realized that I had played the game a few times, and it was never with my copy. I'll happily play but I don't need to own.

RATING: 6
R's  This is a worker placement game that involves set collection, with some thematic twists.  Sets of cubes representing paints of particular colors are purchased in the market.  Particular sets can later be "mixed" into other colors (e.g. yellow+red=orange).  Sets of colors are used to purchase tiles in the cathedral.  This reveals a section of the fresco that the paints have been used to create.  Workers placement is performed secretly and simultaneously, except for one.  This worker is used to show how early the apprentices are forced to begin their day.  Start time has a direct relationship with turn order resolution and relates indirectly with paint prices.  It also has the affect of in/decreasing happiness which can lead to gaining or losing a worker.

RATING: 8.5
This is a resource management game that is remarkably clever in that it's easy to understand, but your choices are complex.  The mechanism of wake-up time is a great innovation, and the mixing of colors is a strong thematic choice.  On the whole I think this is a good, solid game that will have broad appeal.

The modules for this game integrate very nicely, and once you've tried them it's hard to imagine never using them again.  The tertiary colors are a no-brainer, and the additional choices you face with the portraits and the special requests are interesting.  The game as a whole is much improved and now I want to play it more often!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This is very flavorful and thematic game which does very well in simulating the work of renewing the fresco in Renaissance church. There game mechanics are nicely applied to the theme - you will buy colors at the marked, mix them to create another colors, painting not only the fresco wall at the church, but also personal portraits of some nobles from the city to gain more income (yes, even in Renaissance guys have second jobs :) ) and after work you can visit theater to relax.
Sound interesting? For sure. This game is very interesting. 

What I like:
- Wake time selection mechanism is great. It serves both as the leader issue elimination (wake time is chosen from the least points to the highest points player) and also as interesting strategic decision (do I wake early, when everything at the market, fresco, etc. is still available, but the prices are high and I will be tired (morale will decrease)? Or should I sleep longer, be fresh, but I would probably need to take the rest of stuff nobody other wants - although for cheaper?)
This is one of the main attractions of the game
- The AI player (Leonardo) is designed very cleverly. It is used in 2 player games (my only game player count), each player controls him every second round and it serves nicely to block in all parts of the game (wake time selection, market, fresco, portraits, etc.) This adds even more variables into wake time selection (sorting A, B and Leonardo players according to the points and depending who controls Leonardo this turn).
- Game is modular - modules are sort of mini expansions which can be added into game independently to each other and provide wider game experiences. This idea is good and everybody can choose his own favorite combination (do you wanna play gateway game, add only few modules. Are you ready for richer and heavier experience? add more of them). I never tried game without any module as It was not recommended in forums (and I believe the game would be boring and with no interesting decisions). But after several games with only the base three modules (1 - The Portraits, The Bishop’s Request and Special Blend Colors ) I added also two of them from the expansion (8 - The Bells and 9 - The Wall Fresco) which was the sweet spot for me now.
- The theme is original and appealing for lot of non-gamer people. Also the game looks nice.

What I dislike:
- the cartoon tiles (market goods, tiles of fresco) are not very well produced. The tiles tend to get unstuck and overall they do not endure much.
- The setup and tear down could be a bit longer (according to the modules used). But this is usual games like this
- The game play may feel repetitive without many different strategies, it feels more tactical than strategic. This can be changed by adding some more modules, but still the core of the game is to buy colors, mix them and use them for painting the fresco. Repetitive, but it works somehow.

RATING: 7
The basic game is OK, but a bit too simple for me to stay interested long - and the competition for wake-up times seems so critical that I suspect this might be one of those timing-games where you want to stockpile potential and make sure you're (barely) in last place, then surge to first just as the game ends. (Some people love this dynamic - witness Power Grid - but I'm less fond of it.)

The included expansions add enough breadth-of-problem-space to make the game much more interesting to me, and differentiate player positions more (eg: player A has mixed pink; player B has an income of an Orange per round; etc) which improves the ability to guess what other players might do, and in turn plan a course to avoid being cut off by those plans. This makes the game a bit more strategic, and the order of choosing turn order just before endgame less critical.

RATING: 8
First time playing a game with the worker placement mechanic.  Very beautiful looking pure Eurogame about Renaissance era artists in Italy painting the ceiling of a cathedral.  The re-imagining of the ubiquitous wooden blocks as pigments used to mix paint is cute and ingenious.  Game also employs some simultaneous action by having every player plan his worker placement behind screens at the same time - a very interesting mechanic that I found quite fun.  I find most eurogames to some degree mathematical exercises, though and this was no exception - but the game is so short (about 1 hr.) and has such interesting decision making that I would never turn down a game.  I came in 2nd place my first game!  I was a bit stunned as I was completely lost as to the proper strategy my first turn.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.9
Supposing i had the need to show someone which are the virtues of modern "eurogames", i would suggest a game of Fresco.
Almost all the key elements of an eurogame are present here: the work placement, the set collection, the simultaneous action selection (programmed on player's mat), the grid movement, the bid (when choosing market), etc.
Fresco remains underated by core gammers because, i guess, they  view it as "too light": once the players get aquainted with it's mechanics, the game runs smoothly and can be over in just 60-90 min. 
Well, for me this is exactly the main virtue of this game: to be able to offer "so much" in such a compact and elegant form!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Involves acquiring materials (paint, in this case) and converting that material into VPs. There is a big advantage to going earlier in turn order, but a drawback in that it may cause you to lose painters (your workers) and will also cost you more to buy paint at the market.

Although the box is much larger than it should be, the game board is pleasing to look at, and players expose the cathedral fresco as the game progresses, which is a nice feature.

RATING: 8
We played the basic game and were stupefyingly bored. We played again with the first three expansion modules and were ... enchanted, or close to it. A central decision in the game is whether to ensure you go early in the turn order at the cost of paying more for paint and possibly losing an action. Without the modules, going early matters a lot in only one of the five areas of the board (and matters a little in one other area); with the modules, turn order makes a difference in four areas, so the trade-off decision and game are much more interesting.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A light and simple worker-placement game where you collect sets of colored cubes, and then turn them in for points.  There is some jockeying for turn order, which affects market availability and prices.  So, there's a big chunk of this game which feels already familiar.  Workers are placed secretly behind your own screen, so there is no action blocking or fear of not getting something you want.   This might make it more approachable for families, but it lowers the tension level quite a bit.   The happiness meter allows you to possibly gain or lose an extra worker on a given round.   I prefer Agricola, or Stone Age, or Tribune to this, but I can see why it was an SdJ nominee.   Other than the color blending, the theme isn't quite as pervasive as what I've read in other reviews.  The end game snuck up on us quickly, so I'd have to play it some more to see if that's always an issue.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I'm usually not one to care to much about taxonomy, but this here is not worker placement. It's simultaneous action selection using action points (we're not placing workers on limited public spaces so it's not worker placement) with a hidden programming element and a lovely decision trade-off for turn order. 

The game gets increasingly brain burny as it goes on but doesn't become overloaded. Rather, the decisions become increasingly agonizing, particularly which turn order spot to take and the corresponding prices for things and effect on your potential action points (you can get fewer or more depending on where your "mood" marker is). The decisions on which actions to take each turn -- which are programmed secretly then revealed -- make for lots of angst, and you will waste actions from time to time. There are times to hang back and secure first choice for turn order and there are times to go for broke and get the points before they're all gone. The decision space pulls you in enough directions that you never have a dull "auto-pilot" turn. And the game is reasonably thematic; you have to mix colors in order to get secondary and tertiary colored paints.

On the downside, the game is fairly basic and needs some expansions to add some dimensions to it. No problem there as they have up to 10 now, however, that means you need to make yourself a cheat sheet for how to integrate them and keep them all straight.

RATING: 4.5
This was on my wishlist for so long and then I finally got to play it.

There is no way to catch back up to, or indeed really hinder a run-away leader.

The choose time to get up and adjust mood track was the part of the game that interested me the most but it fell completely flat.... there is no reason whatsoever that the first player would not automatically take 7:00 start as it doesn't really affect mood (-1) and the cost to purchase is cheap (2) Getting up earlier affects mood more and makes everything cost more.

There is no urgency for acquiring paints that would warrant paying so much more per tile, and the change of mood track is so long that in our game it was completely irrelevant, as nobody got near either the top (to gain an apprentice) or near the bottom (to lose an apprentice).

The action planning stage is a little strange as you have so many apprentices you can do all the actions.
Closing down a market stall costs you the action and therefore doesn't seem to work because, why would you send your guy there to close down the stall when he could just buy paint instead? Purchasing tiles simply fell to purchasing the tile with the most paints on irrelevant of colours as you could just mix them yourself anyway.

The fresco ceiling grid is small and so its difficult to stop a player gaining the bonus from the bishop being present on the tile when its painted and the victory points awarded seems like they were plucked out of the air randomly and so after 2-3 rounds a runaway leader had painted several 11 cost tiles and thus was so far ahead that we couldn't catch him and had no real way to slow him down.

The gain 3 money action was always a must as that covers the cost you just spend on paint that turn and so it wasn't a real choice about putting a guy there we all just did because you had to if you wanted to continue to play the game.

The improve mood action is really disappointing as it moves you 2 back up the track and thus negates the whole 'penalty' for getting up early.

The end of round gain money equal to the tile painted is a win more mechanic as you gain more money to buy more paint which means you paint more tiles which means you get more money.

Nothing really new or inspiring in this mediocre worker placement game.

RATING: 6
A friend described this as Colonia in less than half the time, and I think that's a pretty good description.  It's basically gather cubes each round, possibly convert them to something else, and then try to get victory points for fulfilling a specific combination.  Most of the game is hoping you get to use your resources in the most efficient manner possible before someone else does and you're left with a bunch of stuff you don't really need.  There's a couple of interesting elements, but I'm just not a fan of a "hope you don't waste too many resources" type of game.  Ultimately whether I'm interesting in playing this comes down to how fast the game plays with experience.  If it's under 45min then it should be acceptable. Bombay comes to mind for sort of targeting the same audience/time-frame, but being a lot more intriguing.  Apparently the only way to play is the advanced version, though, so I'll have to give this another shot.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Fresco is no role model in innovative game design, that's for sure. In fact, mechanically it is about as derivative as you can get with the minor exception of the morale track that rewards players for taking late turns and punishes those who always go first.

The aim is to gather resources (paints) and turn them into victory points as efficiently as possible. Somewhat unusually, this includes a very strong timing component. The closer you get to the end of the game the more important turn order becomes, and sometimes you will just want to hang back in order to get first dibs next turn.

Even though Fresco doesn't bring anything new or substantially different to the table, it presents a very polished medium weight experience with well-integrated mechanics and a convincing theme (even if painting a cathedral may not be the most exciting setting imaginable).

The inclusion of the three (tiny) so-called expansion modules in the box doesn't score the game any extra points. Outside of a family setting they are absolutely required and therefore to be considered part of the base game.

RATING: 7
Fun worker placement game! I would say that it doesn't do anything really different from other games of its type, but everything is held together quite nicely and its a very tight and easy-to-work system, so I like it. I also like that the theme is not often used, so it adds to the freshness.  While this is a WP game, the idea of hiding your workers during placement is smart and is what makes this game feel different. I also like how players jockey for player position. All in all, a nice WP game for those who are in to those types of games (which I'm happy to say: I am!) If I have any one detracting point, it's that it can feel a little repetitive by the end as there is really not that much feeling of development in the game (even with the painting coming clearer as the game goes on!)

RATING: 8.5
Colorful and thematically rich resource management game that could aspire at taking the crown from Stone Age in the light-medium WP category. Simple mechanics but full of hard decisions: from choosing the wake-up time to deciding which places your apprentices will visit, from balancing money and resources this is one of the best worker placement games out there. For the mechanisms to work flawlessly the three expansion modules are mandatory!

EDIT: as of Nov 2013 I own
- Expansion Modules 4, 5, 6
- Expansion Module 7: The Scrolls
- Expansion Modules 8, 9, 10
- Promo: The Bishop's Favor

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I have been VERY pleased with Fresco. Finally a cube pusher that has a theme that makes me want to play. I think the addition of the 3 'expansion' modules add variety as well as alternative paths to victory. The wake up mechanic is very interesting and can be tense. 

2 things are holding my rating back:

1. Being in first place can be devestating. I find it frustrating that you WANT to stay behind and it is very easy to do so. There are incentives to paint frescos early, but I don't think the incentives are enough.

2. I think too much of the game relies on the last 1-2 turns. Everything comes down to player order (see complaint #1) and winners and losers are determined by who paints what frescos in which order. Early in the game you can compensate for someone paining 'your' fresco. There are plenty of others. Late in the game you can not. You are simply out 4-8 points. This can decide the winner.

I would like to see, with more plays, if these complaints intensify or wane. Maybe I will find ways to use these mechanics to my advantage. Maybe I just don't like them.

RATING: 8
I really enjoyed this cube-management, pseudo-worker-placement game. And I'm kinda disappointed that it did not win SDJ in 2010. 

It has a nice theme, which fits the mechanisms very well, and has interesting decisions. It's also not wholly worker placement, since you use simultaneous action selection and you can't really get blocked from performing the actions you chose.. with the possible exception of the market action. The turn order selection mechanism is very nice too... having to balance between performing actions early and paying more at the market and sacrificing worker morale.

I wasn't totally convinced by the rule regarding scoring, where, if you land on the same space as someone else, you choose to either go forward or backward by 1 point (which affects turn order picking order).. and it didn't help that I finished second solely as a result of this rule in the final scoring.

Overall.. an excellent family strategy game that should have won the SDJ award. I certainly look forward to playing it again and trying out some of the extra modules.

RATING: 6
Without the expansions, a rather prosaic game. With them, balancing what you want to do with what you are actually able to do is interesting and some tough choices. Everything stems from picking when to wake up, and sometimes I feel that that is the only really significant choice on some turns, but not most of them by any stretch. Doing each phase in turn order keeps everyone involved, no waiting through stretched out player turns. Definitely mid-light euro, plays in 60-90mins.

The theme and graphics are lovely, and everything ties in nicely - what you are doing generally makes sense given the theme. The sizing of the cubes proportional to value is a nice touch.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a simple, straightforward game, that happens to be very immersive in theme. You are a master painter, trying to paint the ceiling of some church (think Sistine Chapel). However, actually you are in charge of five apprentice workers who do your bidding. The turn order is a novel and humorous mechanic of bidding for wake-up time. Wake up early, and you get first shot at the paint ingredients and painting the ceiling. However, you piss your apprentices off; piss them off too much, and one of them quits. On the other hand, if you wake up later, your apprentices are happier, and soon enough, an additional worker shows up, but you have poorer choice in paint and opportunities. The base game makes for an excellent gateway game, while I feel the expansions should satisfy veteran games (we have yet to get to these). Overall, an excellent game that pairs well with its sibling, Samarkand, from the same company, Queen Games.

Update (10/31/10): Finally played with the three expansions, which adds more variety and depth to gameplay. Accordingly, the points scored are substantial compared to the base game. There are new strategies to examine and play time is somewhat increased, satisfying those who want more meat in their games.

Update (01/14/12): It doesn't get played as much now, and it certainly had a shorter honeymoon than its sister Samarkand, which was bought at the same time. Some complain about the randomness of the market, but I think it is probably the repetitive nature of the game: waking up, going to the market, painting the cathedral ceiling, painting some portraits, mixing the paint, and relaxing at the theater. It's too much like real life, with a very narrow band of variety. I'm downing this to a '7' from an '8'.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Los jugadores son maestros pintores que usan a sus aprendices para restaurar un fresco de una catedral renacentista. Se levantan a distintas horas del día (=eligen su orden de turno), algo que afecta a su humor(=productividad), y el precio de la pintura en el mercado (=lo que pagan por adquirir cubos de recursos).

Durante su turno, pueden comprar pinturas (=recursos), mezclarlas para obtener colores distintos(=recursos para adquirir losetas más valiosas), pintar el fresco (=conseguir PV´s), pintar retratos (=obtener dinero) o ir al teatro para mejorar su humor. Se pueden enviar hasta 5 de sus trabajadores, con un máximo de 3 por acción.

La mecánica principal es precisamente la colocación de trabajadores en su tablero personal, algo que es simultáneo y oculto a los demás y que, como se actúa en función del orden de turno seleccionado (= hora de levantarse dentro de la temática del juego), ocasiona que a veces hayas elegido alguna acción inútil para cuando te toca actuar o que sacrifiques demasiado para conseguir algo que necesitas.

Me gusta la idea de mezclar colores, porque es algo que le sitúa por encima de la típica mecánica de "tomar cubos de recursos para transformarlos en PV´s".

Otra idea brillante es la de seleccionar, en la medida de lo posible, el orden de turno. Levantarse temprano ocasiona que compras la pintura que más necesitas, pero más cara y empeora tu humor (pudiendo llegar a perder trabajadores por ello), levantarse más tarde hace que la pintura del mercado sea más barata, pero no hay tanto dónde elegir, aunque no se resiente tu humor.

Como vemos, combina colocación (oculta) de trabajadores + gestión de recursos + selección simultánea de acciones y además existe un elevado número de formas de conseguir PV´s: dedicarse a las pinturas baratas y convertirlas en PV´s gracias al altar, pintar frescos baratos para luego puntuar por monedas al acabar la partida, ir a por las partes del fresco más caras, mover convenientemente al obispo...

PROS: Tiempo del juego muy ajustado. Accesible para no jugones (basta con no incluir ninguna de las tres expansiones de la caja básica). Configurable, gracias a añadir una, dos o tres de sus expansiones.

CONS: Sin ninguna expansión es un juego muy soso para el jugón medio. El cuarto jugador suele elegir posiciones muy extremas para levantarse (muy tarde o muy temprano). Eurogame típico ejemplo de muevecubos sin alma. Jugando a 2 jugadores, se emplea un jugador ficticio y eso a mi no me gusta demasiado.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Almost a perfect game! Everyone just loves the theme. The production is all up to the high Queen Games standard- thick cardboard, chunky bits, bright colors, a near-perfect rulebook, and my god... that awesome-looking box insert! Great!

The game is perfect as a gateway without adding in the three modules the base game includes. Very very simple to understand and play. The modules all add really good stuff. I would either play with all three of the modules or none, personally.

The modules:
Potraits- excellent! I love some player powers. It also adds a bit more intrigue to the portrait action.

New Paints- wonderful! weighing out whether or not you should go for these high scoring but difficult to get paint colors is a lot of fun.

Income...point...whatever tiles- These are ok. They give you a neat option toward the end of the game and encourage you to maybe take some tiles from the board you wouldn't normally do.

This game is just shy of a masterpiece for me. It would be a 10 if the two player game was a little less... blech.

I finally played the game again and I now feel I was way too harsh with it. When I played it, I liked most games so I’m not sur why I disliked Fresco so much. I recently played the base game with all the modules it has and I have to recognize that even with the modules the game remains pretty light.

The rules of the game are pretty easy to teach, you can do it under 8 minutes. 

The game´s decisions aren’t hard, but they are, somewhat, fun!

Bottom line, Fresco is a light game. I will sell the Bog Box Edition as soon as I can. I have to recognize that Fresco is a great family game, but it is way too light for my current tastes.

RATING: 7
I intentionally avoided Fresco for a year after it came out, figuring it would be too light and not my type of game.

It is a streamlined cube-converter with interesting decisions at both the worker-allocation and turn order decision points.  For a 1-hour game I don't find it light at all, and that's a good thing for me.  (I think) it manages to avoid scriptedness and the tradeoffs between going early and late seem to be balanced.

Whether I have more than 5 plays of Fresco in me I can't say, but I'm confident that I do have 5 given enough opportunity, which is a pleasant surprise.

RATING: 5
As a wargamer at heart I have (in jest) dismissed the generic German cube shuffler as 'buy red cubes to combine with yellow cubes to generate orange cubes to buy victory points - most efficient cube shuffling wins'.

I thought I was being ironic; little did I know I was actually decribing Fresco to a T. I could also have added that there's little point in trying to do too well before the last couple of turns as there's a catch up mechanism that favours players at the back of the pack, and that ideally the theme will be completely irrelevant as it's essentially a mathematical efficiency exercise.

To be fair it's by no means a bad example of the breed and is certainly very pretty, but there's nothing new here at all.

RATING: 6
I'm not exactly sure why this game is described as "worker placement". I feel that the WP mechanic is rooted in the idea of simultaneously selecting an action and preventing an opponent from selecting it. It's true that Fresco has "apprentices" that you place on actions, but they are your own menu of actions, not a shared pool of actions. It feels more like the workers are action points and all actions cost 1 point. I don't get it.

Oh, the game? It's not really compelling, but it's a tightly enough designed set-collecting game to be enjoyed by a light-medium audience. Actually the most interesting aspect of the game is how they market the "full" vs. the "basic" game. The 3 "included expansions" together basically comprise the full game and are not really expansions at all. The game board is already designed to accommodate them for cryin' out loud. It's the basic game where sections of the board are put to no apparent purpose other than holding cubes. It will be interesting to see if other games follow this approach to scaling rule sets for different audiences.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Fresco, the latest totally-not-worker-placement-but-when-has-that-stopped-anyone-before game, puts players in charge of a crew of artisans working to restore a Renaissance fresco in the dome of a cathedral. Each interlocking piece is easy to understand on its own though not particularly novel; however the overall design ticks like a swiss watch and the integration of theme puts much of the competition to shame.

At the start of the game, tiles showing different combinations of paints are randomly distributed in the center of the board, completely covering the un-restored fresco. The goal is simple: purchase paints, mix the appropriate colors, and claim the matching tiles, revealing a fresh, vibrant fresco piece by piece. To outshine the rival artisans, players must carefully manage their actions, resources, morale, timing, cash, income, and turn order, the last of which is especially crucial. A turn begins with players choosing when to begin their workday (5:00-9:00AM). Early birds are heavily rewarded: first crack at the paints available for sale, first choice of tiles to restore, first choice of portraits to complete (for which they will be paid cash and given special privileges), and first crack at accepting special requests from the Bishop (augmenting income). Being first is hugely advantageous in Fresco, but starting the workday at 5:00AM will not go over well with the artisans for very long, eventually costing the player an entire action as productivity sinks among the dreary mood. Indeed, players content to begin their day at 9:00AM may gain a bonus action from high morale, but the best use of the actions will go to their task-master competitors. Turn-order in Fresco strongly recalls Power Grid, as players may hang back to score big, which then places them at the back of the line.

My expectations for this were low (see figure 2 above), but I have to say that this design comes out swinging. Attention to theme is extraordinarily high, if not without fault. The mechanics mesh well and hint at a master's touch. Players are rewarded with a slight edge in income for restoring low-value, base-paint portions of the Fresco. The game encourages a degree of specialization via the Bishop's requests, which players can only access by using some paints more heavily than others. Paints themselves are not quite symmetrical in value. Players who sync up their restoration plans with the bishop's inspections will attain a slight bonus to their scores. High replayability stems from multiple sources: random initial setup, randomized distribution/availability of paints every round, semi-random requests for portraits, etc.

After two plays I can say that Fresco has a lot of heart. I don't think it has much staying power, but it's charming enough that I'd happily play now again. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
It's a pretty good worker placement game, but it's also a lot like other worker placement games, except you're that slave driver Titian instead of some anonymous noble who's trying to build a medieval city.

Maybe I'm jaded, but as smooth and well-done as this game is, it's didn't grab me, either. You know you're bored with this kind of game when the most interesting thing about it is that when you land on someone else's space on the victory track, you get to choose to move one space ahead or one space behind them.

I've just gotten to a point where I question whether games like this need to be made. You know, the Caylus-Pillars of the Earth-Notre Dame family of worker placement games. The theme is mostly represented by the need to combine primary colors. Who cares?

RATING: 7
Only played the basic game. This game is probably closer to an 8, but only having played it once I am reserving judgment. I absolutely love the time mechanic and the way in which score dictates turn position, akin to Power Grid. Having to choose whether to take more points or less points than an opponent makes for an extra layer of strategy and makes for a heavier game in a light frame. Managing happiness adds even more to the excitement. So many great game mechanics. I wish some of the blockbuster titles out there would have borrowed more from this game as they would be even better. Want to play more and hope I do. Not sure if the advanced game adds more to the game, or just adds useless features.

RATING: 6
Very fun "family" game with great components and includes 3 expansions that can be added to add some depth to the game play.  After one play without the expansions, I now play with all of them and I think it certainly adds to the enjoyment. The wake-up time mechanic adds a nice element for impacting mood and cost of paints in the market as well as having a big impact on turn order during each round. After a few plays, the game time is relatively quick. It becomes important to pay attention to what other players are getting in the workshop and what request tiles they are going for so you can plan accordingly. The bishops bonus along with the cards in the portrait gallery can all add up to a lot of additional points over the course of the game. I have played it several times with 3 and 4 and both have been fun. The variable set up (with the expansions) should help with replayability and the modular expansions will allow most groups to step up the level of depth to their liking. Overall, a recommended game for its weight.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game was thoroughly enjoyable.  The turn order mechanic of get-up-time is great.  Get up really early, pay the most for paints and apprentice morale goes down.  Get up late, paints are cheap, not much selection, but morale goes up.  Tough balancing.  I also like that the painting concept uses the colors the way they work in real life with the primaries (red, yellow and blue) being used to make secondary colors of purple, orange and green.  There is a nice tension in trying to get the paints you need in the market and then to complete parts of the Fresco that you have paints for before your opponent does.  Money has to be managed wisely as well as planning your mixing of blended paints (orange, green and purple). Mixing blended paints occurs at the end of the day for the following day , so you have to think ahead a little.  Looking at the expansions, I think that all of them will add an new dimension to the game.  Really excited to teach this to other people.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
If you get it, get the Fresco Big Box release late 2014

In Fresco, players are master painters, working to restore a fresco in a Renaissance church.

Each round begins with players deciding what time they would like to wake up for the day. The earlier you wake up, the earlier you will be in turn order, and the better options you will be guaranteed to have. Wake up early too often, however, and your apprentices will become unhappy and stop working as efficiently. They would much rather sleep in.

RATING: 8
A nice little euro, reminiscent of other titles but still interesting. Reminds me most of Royal Palace in that you will be "buying" tiles off of a main board shared by the table. RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Although a cube shuffling game at heart, it's cleverly hidden behind the theme of restoring a cathedral fresco by buying, mixing, and using paints. In this case, the cube format works well---a yellow cube stands for yellow! Red is red and blue is blue, and so forth. You don't have to pretend that a cube is some arbitrary resource, and when you mix them together, they behave as you would expect by generating new colors.

Beyond the typical cube shuffling, the wake-up mechanism in Fresco is neat. It's nice that you now have control over your turn---is it better to go first in turn order or try to increase your mood to gain an extra worker? However, the wake-up mechanism only really works if you play with all of the modules. Without the bonuses provided by the Portraits or the Bishop's Requests, there is almost no strong reason to wake up early and suffer a bad mood. You need to have some incentive to wake up early to make that initial decision more tense, otherwise you end up settling with a predictable pattern of wake up times over and over again. Another aspect that we found missing was that there wasn't really any tactical play around which tiles to restore on the fresco. Not only that, I wish there was a little more control over the movement of the bishop, rather than having him just appear at each finished tile (we found that the "private jet" portrait that lets you fly the bishop anywhere to be very powerful). I have high hopes for some of the other expansions such as the Scrolls or Glaziers that provide players with incentive to restore different portions of the fresco. 

We enjoyed it with three players and the simultaneous worker placement made for little downtime. People have compared two-player [thing=66188][/thing] with two-player [thing=6249][/thing] due to the dummy player, but we found it pretty different. The Dirk dummy player in [thing=6249][/thing] isn't really a player---it's more of a tile dump that you can use to change the positioning of your opponent. The Leonardo dummy player in [thing=66188][/thing] is more like a real player taking real actions, and it's a little clunky to pass him back and forth. Moreover, Leonardo is fairly aggressive---since he doesn't actually accumulate resources, his only purpose is to shut down paint stalls, steal portraits, or generally grab other items from your opponent. We were okay with it, but I can see how some couples who prefer non-aggressive play would dislike this. In [thing=6249][/thing], you still have to at least use your own resources to buy a tile for Dirk. There's always some opportunity cost to balance aggressive, blocking play, whereas in [thing=66188][/thing], the sole pupose of Leonardo is blocking. We'll probably keep it in our rotation, but it does feel better with more.

RATING: 7
Excellent resource management game where you are trying to paint the ceiling of a chapel.  You first wake up to determine your turn order.  Then you acquire paints at the market.  You then go on to placing your workers behind a screen to determine what you will do that turn.  You can mix paints, paint the chapel, acquire cards to provide benefits, etc.  There are several expansions that add a lot of replayability to the game.  Light to medium weight Euro that is a good "next step" game.

RATING: N/A
Das Leben eines Freskenmalers in der Renaissance ist nicht einfach. Da ist einerseits der Bischof, der unbedingt sein Fresko und seinen Altar renoviert bekommen möchte, der aber andererseits die Arbeit erst bezahlt, wenn man sie geleistet hat. Und wie soll man sich die teuren Farben leisten, die man dafür benötigt. 
Zum Glück hat jeder noch einige Gehilfen zur Verfügung, die auf dem Markt Grundfarben einkaufen, diese dann zu den benötigten Farben zusammenmischen und das Fresko renovieren. Dabei gilt: Je früher man aufsteht, desto größer sind auch auf dem Markt die Chancen, die passenden Farben zu finden, wobei leider morgens noch echte Wucherpreise zu bezahlen sind. Und wie das so ist mit dem frühen Aufstehen ... Man hat den ganzen Tag schlechte Laune und wenn man die zu sehr an seinen Gehilfen auslässt, machen die sich aus dem Staub. Da hilft nur, abends die Laune bei einem Theaterbesuch wieder aufzupäppeln. Wenn dann da nur nicht wieder das frühe Aufstehen wäre ...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Definitely not a bad worker placement game.  What does it bring to the table?  Selecting what time you'll wake up (turn order) and how that affects your mood is fun.  get too crabby from waking up too early and people stop wanting to work with you (lose workers).  Go see a play at the theater to lighten up, wake up late a couple of mornings, and suddenly your cheerful enough to get more people wanting to work for you.  I also like that you're selecting all of your placements in secret.  None of this, "well you went there, so I guess I'll change my plan and go here."... No, you're going to commit to all the spots you'll go for the round.  Quite agonizing and fun!  I only wish it played down to 2 players without some sort of "dummy" player mechanic.

RATING: 7
Fresco is a gorgeous game with some nifty elements to it, particularly the wake-up and mood tracks. It was easy to learn, fun to play, and my first game resulted in a very tight final score grouping -- just two points between first and second, and another two between second and third.

The screens are a fun addition, and the constant tug-of-war between wanting to go first and not wanting to lose mood or pay through the nose for things was enjoyable. This interplay made for some tough decisions, and I often made a choice and realized a few moments later that it had been the wrong one.

Probably bears another play to really get a good feel for it; this could turn into an 8. As it is, I wasn't blown away but I had fun and would happily play it again.

RATING: 7
Fresco is a worker placement game.  In it players are renown Renaissance-era master painters, working on completing the ceiling fresco of a great cathedral.

Each round is a day.  First you will decide when you will wake up.  The time you wake up determines turn order.  The earlier you get up, the more choices you'll have, but you will make your apprentices unhappy, and they may leave you.  Sleeping in will make your apprentices happy, but you take your turn later, and will have fewer choices available.  Your apprentices are the workers that you place out on the board.  You can send them off to market to purchase paints, or to mix the paints you have to make other colours, or to do side jobs to make you money, or even to the theatre to improve moral.  Then if you have the proper colours you will paint sections of the fresco.  This will get you points, and whoever has the most points at the end of the game wins.

I enjoy Fresco quite a lot!  Part of what makes this a fairly unique worker placement is the implementation of a little used mechanic.  It's where players all choose what their workers are going to do at the same time.  Other aspects make it an enjoyable experience too.  Things like getting various paints, represented by coloured cubes, and mixing them to get more exotic paints.  The board is also beautiful.  Watching the fresco come to life, through the removal of tiles to reveal it as it gets "painted", is somehow fascinating.

If you're only going to get one worker placement game, this very well might be the one.  If you like worker placement games in general, then you should surely have Fresco in your collection.  I highly recommend it.

RATING: 7
01/05/13 Played a three-player game in two hours. We used the expansion that added brown and fuchsia paints. 
My impression of Fresco grew as I played. Initially when I heard we'd be mixing paints (ex. blue + yellow = green) I thought, "What? Am I in third grade?" But I soon found out there was more to the game than that. It has a unique theme that pretty much fits the mechanics. The illogic of you painting tiles and *then* mix paints was the game's way of making you wait a turn to use that mixed paint; an additional turn where someone else might "get thar furstest with the mostest." That added some tension; so you had to keep an eye on what colors the other players were mixing. (A memory element as players kept their paints and money behind a screen.) Where I had assumed a game that wouldn't work my brain, I found it often to be a brain burner with occasional analysis paralysis. The rules were not complex but the planning stage could give you fits as you tried to have not only a plan A, but a plan B & C in case you were thwarted getting the paints you wanted. Direct interaction is low. Most player interaction is trying to guess the other's plans and grab the resources they want first. I was pleasantly surprised one player, who is colorblind, did *not* have a problem with this game that was all about recognizing colors. I've played several other "collect these color cubes and turn them in for VPs" type games, but this is one of the better ones. One reason, this one made sense theme-wise. Yes, you really do need blue and red paint if you want to use purple. I rated it a "7". That might go up or down as I see what the other expansions (portraits and bishop's request) in the box do in future games.   
03/12/13 Played a three-player game in 1.3 hours. We used the Special Blends expansion module. 05/07/13 Played a three-player game in 1.3 hours. We used the Special Blends and Portraits expansion modules. 02/25/14 Played a three-player game in 1.75 hours. We used the Special Blends and Portraits expansion modules. 06/14/14 Played a three-player game in 1.6 hours. We used the Special Blends and Portraits expansion modules. 03/21/15 Played a three-player game using the special blends module. 01/16/16 Played a three-player game using the special blends and Bishop's Request expansion modules in 1.6 hours.

I feel this was a well-made game that would improve with more plays.  It seemed a little simplistic in my first play, although the decisions were weighty and the gameplay was intense throughout.  I'm just not that much of a fan of the "grab and score" mechanic (collecting goods only to immediately convert them into victory points).  I like more options (should I convert to money or VP? should I get VP through this method or a different one?), and I felt this game was somewhat lacking in them.  But it was an enjoyable experience nonetheless, and I'd be willing to play again, but it's unlikely that I would purchase it myself.

[size=10][u][i][b]r{Initial Thoughts}r (after 5 plays):[/u][/b][/i][/size]
* Clever resource collection/worker placement game with a good theme (painting)
* High quality Queen components make the game fun to play and easy to look at
* Game comes with three expansion modules, one of which (portraits) is essential 
* Several expansions have been released, but all are expensive for what they provide
* Ebb and flow of grabbing paint/restoring tiles is unforgiving, especially for newbies
* Great for Eurogamers and art lovers
* Initial rating: [b]8/10[/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
In Fresko verkörpern die Spieler Maler der Renaissance, die ein Kirchenfresko restaurieren sollen. Für diese Aufgabe benötigen die Maler natürlich Farbe, die auf dem Wochenmarkt gekauft werden muss. Doch die Grundfarben allein reichen nicht, denn die Farben muss ggf noch gemischt werden, um die richtigen Farbtöne für das Fresko zu bekommen. Hat man die passenden Farben, kann man die Arbeit an dem Fresko fortführen, oder im eigenen Studio Portraits malen, um an Geld zu kommen. All diese Aufgaben, übernehmen die Gehilfen des Malers, wodürch Fresko schon fast zu einem klassischen Worker-Placement Spiel wird. Dabei hat es aber genügend Eigenständigkeit und ein schönes Thema, um immer mal wieder gerne auf den Tisch zu kommen.

RATING: 7
This is actually a really solid little game. Maybe a little over-produced for what is essentially a game of churning cubes into points, but it is very appealing at least. I was actually expecting this game to be way too simple for my tastes, but this has quickly become a choice gateway game for me. There isn't anything all that innovative here, except for maybe how player order is done, though I had seen that already in Viticulture. In many ways, this feels like Lords of Waterdeep without all the chrome, which is something I can really appreciate.

As for the included expansions, the deck of helper cards are good to throw in. They don't change the game that drastically, and they give you an alternative to just taking money. The bonuses for having 3 types of Fresco tiles aren't that special, in my opinion. They're cool in theory, but don't really come into play very often unless you're playing with less players. The new paint colors, however, are practically essential. They add some extra steps to mixing colors, and this leads to more points. Good stuff.

RATING: N/A
Players are painters who are attempting to fresco the ceiling of a chapel. In player order, they must decide what time to get up (which affects their mood, market prices, and the number of apprentices they can have). They then secretly plot their actions using their apprentices (going to the market, going to the chapel, earning money, mixing paints, or improving their mood. Everyone then reveals, and take their actions. In the market, players can acquire paints In the chapel, players earn VPs by using selected paint combinations. Players can also earn $3 per apprentice taking the money action. Paints can be also mixed, with one apprentice allowing two mixes. Finally, apprentices can also be used to improve your mood. 

We played a key rule wrong, when we interpreted the rule to allow a person to visit more than one market, when during your turn, you are only allowed to viist one market stall. 

So I would like to try again since we have only played one game. The theme is cute, and the game plays decently.

RATING: 8.5
What a lovely game.  The uncommon theme intrigues people, I have found.  Which is good because the base rule set is simple enough to understand that this works well with casual gamers.  The three included modules add just that extra bit of strategy to push this up to a medium weight game when playing with folk who are more familiar with table top games.

The way turns are determined can keep games close if players collaborate against a run away leader, slowing down their options.

I really enjoy this game.  It is not very tense, but it does have moments of focused figuring to make sure you can paint the portion you are hoping to.  Overall a pleasant experience.

Plus for some reason as we move from area to area of the round order my gaming group has developed a habit of saying, "To the cathedral!", or "To the studio!", while imagining a Terry Gilliamesque troupe of masters and their apprentices running together to the different areas of the board.  That never seems to get old.  It stems from someone pointing out that all these people do the same activity at the same time.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
So-so. Fairly high levels of chaos because of simultaneous secret planning (I don't like that aspect). It's mainly set collection, where you trade in sets of cubes for VP tiles, but you might not get the cubes you have planned for, or you do, but someone else buys that tile before you, meaning your sets are wrong. 

Difficult to plan ahead so is mainly tactical. Also there's hidden but trackable info, which I don't like, and think the game would be greatly improved if players could see what others were positioning themselves for, and plan around it.

I liked the mechanic where when you buy a tile you flip it over and keep it for income, and if you get a set of 3 similar ones you get bonus points and cube-income. This is the best part of the game. This made different tiles more valuable to different players, and it's tricky to get the 3rd in a set - you have to actively work at it rather than just sit back and see what cubes you get. 

The get-out-of-bed mechanic was peculiar as there is a trade off between several things. It does produce some weird results: the 4th player might be forced to choose between 2 very different times e.g. 1st or last, or they might have 2 adjacent slots that are identical except that one is a better version of the other. 

The last player has the least choice, but they are relieved from the responsibility of pressuring the next player(s). Similarly the first player has the most choice, but having to commit first means the other players can just flow around you. e.g. player 1 (the losing player) has to choose 5am if he wants to guarantee going first, but player 4 (the winning player) might be able to get up at 6am and still go first.

I'm not convinced the catch up mechanism does what it's supposed to.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A harmless Euro. The first choice each turn is turn order, based on reverse VP order. Going first increases the costs of getting cubes a la New England. Then once you know the order, you've got 5 guys to allocate to jobs (max 3 to any one job), the jobs being get cubes (they've been randomly put out in lots), use cubes to buy VP tiles (you need to correct colour combinations in cubes), swap two cubes in for a bigger brighter one (which are also needed for the VP tiles), grab money, or push up your dude on the whatever-it's-called track that'll get you an extra worker if you reach the top. Repeat until all the VP tiles are gone. The questions each turn are mainly where do I want to be on turn orders (trade off between cost and first choice) and what cubes will others take before me, therefore how many workers do I allocate to cubes. If it's risky, it may be better to allocate workers to the guaranteed jobs (eg get money) and hope to claim a higher turn order position next turn. So , do I play risky and potentially waste a worker, or not? And hope that other people leave you VP tiles you can make. There's not much more to it. It ends in about the right timeframe. Mostly harmless.

RATING: 6
Simple game to understand but contains some very tough decisions.  Deciding when to mix the more valuable colors is challenging.  This game has a theme that is definitely not pasted on; you almost feel like you are in an art class (in a good way).  In fact its been a while since Ive seen a game where the mechanics and the theme flow so well together.  The wake up time used to determine turn order and apprentices' mood works particularly well.  The set up and take down time is a bit of an obstacle.  It can take a good 10 minutes each for set up and take down, so good organization is a must.  The game play can be a tad fiddly, especially with a 2 player game where you each take turns managing the "dummy" player.  Reshuffling and redistributing the market tiles each turn is also a bit "fiddly".  But, regardless, the game works and it is quite a bit of fun.  The three included "expansions" will quickly become a  must.

RATING: 7
Preso quasi solo per l'ottima veste grafica e l'alta qualità dei prodotti Queen Games, alla fine si è rivelato un buonissimo acquisto. 

A difficoltà regolabile (decidi il numero di espansioni in base alle persone con cui lo giochi), Fresco scorre come un fuso grazie a meccaniche semplici: carina l'idea della sveglia (che può rovinarti l'umore e allontanare i tuoi aiutanti) e ben pensata la scelta simultanea delle azioni dei tuoi uomini. La fase di restauro invece non è niente di originale (mi viene in mente Royal Palace, ma ce ne sono mille di giochi con questo meccanismo). 

Nota di merito alla scelta di produrre cubi colorati di diverse dimensioni a seconda della tipologia di colore (primario, secondario e, nell'espansione, terziario): grazie Queen Games, anche un daltonico può giocare!

Un Eurogame tradizionale che rivedrà il tavolo molto spesso.

***

One 4-player game - no expansion added.

Nice components and a well integrated theme. Your actions makes sense, thus the rules are easy to remember also for families.

Not really dry, but a bit of a calculating machine. Some of the actions are also put into the play in a somewhat "reversed" order (yeah, you would love to mix paint BEFORE going to the cathedral). It's about timing and planning your strategy for several rounds ahead.  

I have only one real major negative issue with this game: the freaking catch-up-mechanic. This is a really harsh one, much more dominant than that in Power Grid for example. In reality: once you get ahead, you have practically some "wasted" turns: it is no use place your workers on the market, since those players behind in points would get there earlier to buy it all. You'll just have to wait, get some money from the studios and sweat it out.. I don't like games where you for large parts of the game have no meaningful decisions to make. This should warrant a lower rating, but since this game does other things so well, I wait for more plays (maybe with a expansion as well).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fresco is a nice, pleasant game. Get paint cubes in the primary colors, mix them into secondary colors, then use those cubes - when the timing is right - to paint the fresco and score points. Added bonus-modules give more weight to your decisions.

Fresco's most striking feature is that all the player interaction is concentrated in one point (it's not really a worker placement but a simultaneous action selection game). On every area of the board it's first come, first serve, and the player to go first is the player to 'get up earliest in the morning'. Getting up early has its downsides, that you need to spend actions on to compensate. So early in turn order = less, but better actions, and later = more, but worse actions. The player with the least vp is the first to choose at what time to get up, so it might be wise to hoard cubes and not convert them to points yet... until the point where someone goes before you and takes all the high cube-to-vp conversion tiles.

It's probably a good idea to play with all the expansions (unless you play with people new to the hobby). Without them, it's too easy to wait around and pick whatever's left, instead of making the tough decisions what color to go for and when to convert to vp.

There are not that many avenues or strategies to explore, which is why I won't rate it higher than a 7, but on the flipside you can't really go wrong with any strategy, which makes it a great family game. Getting extra workers is good, having fewer isn't too bad, paying a lot for the good tiles or only a little for the bad ones, it can all work. Not too deep, but a satisfying game nonetheless.

Update: It's suprisingly fun, and the constant tension of the possibility of the other players taking exactly what you wanted just before you could take it, with the hidden simultaneous action selection, make it interactive enough for me. Yes, it's light, but that's not really a weakness. An 8, for now.

RATING: N/A
As Euros and Worker Placement games go, this one is not awful. The theme actually makes sense, especially where mixing paint colors is concerned. The Action Selection also makes sense, as you have the intuitive tradeoffs between waking up early (better choices of action but less pleased workers) vs waking up later (more limited action choices but increased worker happiness). It's a bit weird to cash in your green and red paints to uncover a part of the fresco that has neither green nor red on it, but no matter. The rulebook is clear and short, the included expansions can be added or removed to taste, and overall this is a pretty decent family-weight game. Nothing spectacular, but it's pleasant and clean and [i]works.[/i]
