=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I love the theme. It's pretty unique as far as worker placement games go. But if Fresco only had a good theme, it wouldn't be more worthwhile than any of the other worker placement games that I already have and enjoy. The interesting twist that Fresco adds is the simultaneous action selection. So much of the tension in the majority of worker placement games comes from blocking people from taking the actions that they want. But in Fresco, players can place their workers where ever they want to determine  their actions.  

During a turn, a player can buy paint, paint at the church for VP, paint portraits for money, mix paints to make more colorful art and go to the theatre to feel happier. So a player can send as many of her five people to paint in the church as she would like (limit of three), but the tiles may or may not be available when it becomes her turn to decide which tile to paint. You assign all of your workers before learning where the other players have assigned all of their workers, so there is always a chance of placing a worker somewhere that it will be useless. It's a clever mechanic, and one that involves a decent amount of planning.

I have only played with the expansions and I am only interested in future plays with the expansions.  The expansions allow different players to each pursue a different overall strategy.  There is a lot less depth without them and the game becomes mostly cube pushing.  But the expansions come in the box, and it's a neat idea to have a number of different ways to play the game all in one box.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
There's enough meaningful choices throughout Fresco to keep things interesting and the production values are excellent. Great game to look at. I wouldn't recommend playing without the modules as they add a lot of interesting choices to the game.

Fresco seems to fit into a wierd category of games.. It's very light for adults but pretty heavy for children. 

My biggest complaint may be that the game enforces too much balancing of scores between players. Lots of power in being first player, but player order is based on score. I would advise a variant where player bid for first player (players tend to have too much money anyway).

EDIT: After implementing a bidding variant I enjoy this game a lot more. Much less forgiving.

RATING: 6
Very solid worker placement Euro game with a good integration of theme and mechanisms (mixing of paint). I like that you plan the actions of your workers (comparable to Dungeon Lords). Letting your workers sleep in or kick them out of bed early in the morning is an innovative turn order mechanism. 

With the different expansions it scales well from family to gamer's game and also the game length is a bit less than for most heavy Euros. The art work is also great.

I do not like that every game plays out rather the same without much differences. Furthermore the game does not evolve much during one session, since early turns play out rather similar to later turns. But in the end it is still a very solid game.

RATING: 9.5
Very fun, medium Euro with a very well-integrated theme.  The mixing of the colors gives it a real boost over other games where you collect resources and turn them into victory points (e.g. Stone Age), and the constant push-and-pull for turn order (wake-up time) is well-implemented. This may get up to a 10 with more plays.

RATING: 6
Fresco: you use coins to buy colour cubes which you turned into cubes of another colour, and then in turn transform them into VPs. Yes, we've all been here before. 

You are juggling several resources (money, paints, worker satisfaction) and have limited actions (no. of workers) to get the stuff done for the VPs. You have hidden worker placement (behind a screen); though you can't quite block another player from taking an action, if you go earlier, you can make their action less useful for them. This means that going first is useful: but it costs a lot more to do so, and the player ahead in points has less choice about their place in the turn order anyway. So, full of typical Euro balancing mechanisms. 

All in all, it works very smoothly and the various working parts interact logically. I'm not going to object to playing this if tonight's game absolutely has to be a cube-pusher. However, there's nothing really here that you haven't seen before.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The players are master artists utilizing their apprentices to restore a Fresco. Plays wake up at different times of the day, affecting their mood (read: productivity), the price of paint at the market, and the player order. There's a hidden worker allocation thing going on which is very nice, deciding between buying paint at the market which come in randomized sets, restoring a piece of the fresco or using excess paint on the altar, painting portraits for cash or "portrait cards" that grant specific benefits, mixing paints or fulfilling requests for completed sets of fresco sections, and improving your mood.

The simple game uses red, blue, and yellow basic colors, and also secondary colors (green, orange, and purple). You can sometimes get the secondary colors at the market, but more often you will have to mix paints to get the ones you need. The fresco tiles list the color requirements and VP award right on them. The advanced game adds tertiary colors brown and magenta, along with the portrait cards, and the request tiles for additional VP. The portraits add a lot to the game, and should be played with. The tertiary colors aren't too complicated to deal with and just add a little more depth and planning to fresco restoration. The request tiles are ok, I could play with or without them. If you're playing for the first time, listen to the basic rules first, and if it's not overwhelming, go right ahead and add the advanced components for a fuller Fresco experience. The game comes with cheat sheets that make it very clear how paint gets mixed, so don't let all this color talk scare you off.

Completed fresco sections give you income per turn, which you use to buy paints, which you use to paint more frescos. There are several paths to victory here, you could focus on getting cheap paint and dumping it for quick VP at the altar, or paint a lot of cheap frescos (especially plausible in the basic game) for a high income that translate to extra VP at game end. Or you could get up extra early to get the best paints and restore the best fresco sections. Or focus on doing portraits and getting portrait cards. I think you get the point.

I played this twice at Oasis of Fun and would gladly play it again.

RATING: 7
Played multiple times at all player counts. In its most basic form, Fresco is a simple, surprisingly thematic but rather mediocre game. The most interesting decision in the game is choosing the time to wake your workers up, as there are a lot of tradeoffs required to jump ahead in turn order. There is also some forward planning required in the purchasing and blending of paints, which is neat. But what holds the game down is the repetitiveness of actions and the general lack of an arc of progress.

Thankfully, the game comes with 3 expansion modules to increase the variety of gameplay elements, which is recommended once players have gone through the basic game once. More importantly, these modules help break up the repetitiveness by giving additional objectives – tertiary colours, additional income and so on – for players to aim for. The modules don't drastically alter the game for the better, but at least they make Fresco a good choice for those who want a gateway game with some modularity.

A quick word about the 2P game – I find the dummy player mechanism forced. He does make 'intelligent' decisions since he's alternately controlled by players, but he ends up being a mere pawn to benefit the current controller. I wouldn't recommend Fresco as a 2P game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
2017: 8. I picked this up based on some positive review, the alternate take on worker placement, the neat theme (colour theory!) and the hilarious idea of having to decide when to wake up in the morning.

I initially thought it might be a good game to play with my parents but it's just slightly too complex, but it's been a strong hit with my other gaming groups. I've played 4 times but feel like none of those games "count" since I realized we were playing two key rules wrong (you don't get to cherry pick paint from *any* stall, you only get paint from *one* stall; and the painting actions are all separate, not all at once, and the bishop moves to the painted tile after each action) so now I'm even more eager to get the game back to the table and test different strategies and tactics.

Despite this, I think it's still a pretty strong 8, a good mid-weight euro with a unique theme that looks great on the table and is pleasing to a wide range of gamers.

RATING: 7
This is a simultaneous action selection set collection game. The board shows tiles requiring various combinations of paints. Players turn order is initially random, then in reverse score order. Each round begins by players choosing their turn order this round; earlier = pay more for each market item and make your apprentices unhappy (if they're unhappy enough, you lose an apprentice for the turn). Then simultaneously assign your 5 (or 4) apprentices to tasks: buy at market, paint fresco (i.e., collect a tile and collect the associated VP) or work on the altar (turn in a different combination of paints, for fewer VP than most tiles), collect cash, mix paints (primary to secondary, needed for most tiles), or rest (improve happiness). In time order, use your apprentices to conduct their actions. Collect income (1 per tile already completed), then repeat. The final round, triggered by having 6 or fewer fresco tiles available, gives you 2 chances to paint the tiles.

Whenever you score VP, if you land on another player's marker on the track you choose to go in front of them (more VP) or behind (earlier turn order). That's kind of clever, although also seems a bit odd.

This is a nice little game with a lot of decisions to make along the way. But it still plays pretty quickly. Looking forward to trying the advanced versions!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I have the version that comes with the first 3 "modules", or mini-expansions, included.  This is one of the few games that is made more complete with these modules.

The game itself is a simple, and easy to teach and play worker placement game.  Players select their turn order via what time they start work for the day (the earlier you get up the earlier you go but you lose "happiness" which can affect the number of workers you have to place as well as increases your price for buying paint) then secretly select their actions and carry them out based on their turn order.  The end goal being to paint the most (and most valuable) pieces of a fresco.

The theme is really well-done.  Your resources are different colors of paints and you can mix the primary colors to make secondary colors.  I like the idea that getting up early gets you earlier access to all the actions but also costs you happiness and good prices at the market.  This isn't just thematic but also makes for interesting choices in an otherwise by-the-numbers worker placement game.

Where this game really shines though is that this light worker placement game I described above really acts as a framework for which these modules fit perfectly to create a fuller and often more interesting game.  One module adds portraits you can paint for customers that give you a one-time or lasting bonus.  Another gives you the ability to fill orders as another method of scoring bonus points.  The final one adds new tiles and even new paint colors to mix together.

This may replace Lords of Waterdeep for a time as my introductory worker placement game as I'd love to explore the system more.  At least in the interim until Scoundrels of Skullport is released (and I can convince my wife to let me make another game purchase).

It's also worth mentioning that since the resources are based on different colors of paint this isn't the best game to play with color-blind folks.

RATING: N/A
Played the full version with all the expansions. I didn't find the game particularly challenging specially for the time it takes. It's very boring because I had the feeling of doing the same thing over and over. I know that games are all about doing the same repeatedly but some are more obvious than others and Fresko turned out to be one of these obvious ones.

Components are of excellent quality as in Queen standards and the artwork is also very beautiful.

I like some of the mechanisms of the game such as the mood of the players and the turn choice. It's cool to make such a decision and there's no clear choice very often. Most of the time there's a lot to consider before making the decision and I like that.

RATING: 5
Played the basic version twice, and to be honest this game is vastly overrated.  It's not that it is a bad game, it's just not fun.  The play process is extremely mechanical with the player actions having almost no effect on the board, barring increasing along the VP track.  There is some slightly AP which can crop up for some players because of the need to plan for the next turn at the very start of the current turn.

Caveat is that we've played only the basic game so far, and will try again with the 'expansions' but I feel that it is unlikely to change my opinion as I had no problem with the play of the mechanics, only with the tension and excitement in the game.

This is another game in an increasing line of game which add play mechanics to the mix without improving the more important tension in the game.

I have no problem playing this in a group again, but it will never come out by my choice.

RATING: 7

Combinations of colors (got a purple, red and yellow?) were cashed in to score tiles for VP.  You also had to have an income in order to keep buying paint.  Budgeting was a nagging issue from start to finish.  Wonderful game if you enjoy juggling details.  Pile all jolly in for the paint-collecting thing, make sure you get some, for on that all else depends.

RATING: 8.8
2.64
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
SOLD 1/2015 --- The Spiel des Jahres  had yet to be awarded to Dixit at the time, so I jumped at the opportunity to next try one of the top contenders for the award - Fresco. Unfortunately, the Queen demo team wanted to stick to the basic game rather than include any of the "advanced" rules. I had a feeling from reading on the Geek that this was going to make the game too simple to be interesting and that turned out to be exactly right. I’m still interested in trying Fresco again, but with the basic rules the game is simply bland and insipid. The much vaunted choose-when-you-wake-up mechanic, which controls turn order and prices, was not nearly as inspired as had been touted. The game was simply entailed spending coins to purchase cubes and using actions to convert cubes into victory points. I had thought at the time that this might win the Spiel des Jahres since it was time to give the award to Queen, but now I’m glad that something else took the prize. I was personally pulling for A La Carte since Karl-Heinz deserves a "lifetime achievement" award like Knizia, but Dixit isn’t a bad choice and certainly has the expandable element they’re looking for. I had the chance to play both Samarkand and Fresco with Brad Keen of the Dice Tower who is great to game with, and it was nice to be taught both games by Queen staff, but in the end both were a letdown.

Received as gift: November 21, 2010

RATING: 7
[thing=66188][/thing] is a light to medium weight work placement, where you must also decide ahead of time your actions for the turn - considering, after you decide in what hours will your painters wake up, how many you will send to:
- buy paints;
- actually do some painting;
- collect payments;
- blend colors;
- to get time off (which will make them happier and, with this, you might avoid losing workers or even gain more).

You buy paints to use them into paiting the ceiling of the church (the main way to get VPs). Also, the basic colors: yellow, blue and red, can be used to produce green, orange and purple - almost all parts of the paint job require these blended colors. The money you use to buy paints, that, for some reason, are costier early in the morning - maybe your workers don't do research or spend a lot in cofee.

Also, the earlier your workers wake up, more grumpier they will be, and even one or two of them can leave. But if you treat them right, the overall happiness in your shop could attract more workers.

The game is smartly made, all the mechanics work together pretty well, and also the game is beautiful.

There isn't anything major wrong with it, but also nothing really awesome about it. A solid work placement that plays somewhat fast and can be teached in 10 minutes. A good game sure, but there are many ahead of it in the same time frame and weight - like [thing=97207][/thing], [thing=58421][/thing] and [thing=104006][/thing].

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
08/2012:7
A typical worker placement game with players taking turns buying paints to complete a masterpiece on the ceiling of a chapel. There is nothing terribly new in terms of mechanics for this game. The inverse turn order to current leading player in the score track is reminiscent of Power Grid. Familiar elements such as securing initiative at the expense of increased costs and lower morale are elements that can found in many other worker placement games. However this game integrates the mechanics very well with theme. All the actions matches the narrative: waking up early to buy paints in the market to get the best but most costly paints while causing some consternation from the workers makes good sense. Also working to paint the chapel and getting favors from the bishop while getting an external income to support the workers in the mass market feels logical. 

The only gripe I have is the underutilized scoring from painting the altar. It feels like a tacked on scoring mechanism to alleviate the pain of collecting paints but being unable to paint the ceiling tiles during the final round of the game. A consolation price to placate the losing artist for missing out on the main event. Seems like a slight tweak of the rewards for painting the altar should make this more competitive.

RATING: 5
Somewhat interesting game but the gameplay and choices felt a bit loose and was plagued by a bit of randomness.  The game contained a bunch of interesting mechanics which I enjoyed individually but the gameplay as a whole fell pretty flat for me.  

I also personally wasn't a fan of the hidden information as it makes it difficult to plan effectively (hence the "loose" comment above).  It would be quite a serious memory exercise to try and figure out what everyone had, especially as players tend to get impatient and overlap their actions with another players.   Making information/paint open is probably a terrible solution though as I can see this leading to major AP.  

I'm not saying it's a bad game.  It just wasn't for me.  I think the take away point here is that if you like open information and the ability to plan effectively think twice before buying this game.  I would classify this game as more of a push your luck style game than a typical euro or strategy game.  I however, imagine it could be a good gateway game because it doesn't reward deep analysis and can be played intuitively and quickly without too much penalty.  The rules were also simple to learn and the mechanics were fairly intuitive and well tied into the theme.

I would play again if it's all that is going to hit the table at a games night or if all the other proposed options were woeful.  However there are an absolute ton of new games I would rather try or old games I would rather play again over this.

RATING: 7
One play, basic rules. A good medium-weight game, a novel theme, and certainly pretty. I could see this as a Spiel des Jahres nominee.  However it did seem reminiscent of other games, in particular Diamonds Club and Blue Moon City. Like DC you collect resource type A which you to combine to create resource type B and then combine again to obtain VP-yielding C; you can go earlier in player order but your purchases will be more expensive. Like BMC there is a display of tiles which you claim by giving up a resource combination of the right colour, gaining a bonus from moving a non-player piece towards the tile being claimed.

RATING: 7
Also includes [GAMEID=109140] module

I first played this at the UK BGE 2013 when I was randomly "accosted" by the demonstrator as I walked past the table. 

At first as we went through the rules, I thought it was going to be a very boring game (I'm not into art, really, and am definitely not into religion, and it's basically painting a chapel roof. Note: This game is [i]in no way[/i] religious). Once we started playing, I really enjoyed both the theme and the mechanic, and after playing, I hunted high and low for a copy at the expo itself - I was in, and wanted my own straight away. Of course, they had sold out...

Since then I have bought the game, I played it a few times with none of the modules (even those in the main box) for fear of adding complexity with new players. However, I needn't have worried; as noted in the plays list, while the expansions add complexity for complete beginners to the game, they're not so complex as to break anything, and the game ran very well. 

I've backed the big box on KS, and am anxiously awaiting my new copy and new board :D

Traded for Discworld: Ankh Morpork at the UK BGE 2014

RATING: 9.25
High level of family fun.  Easy to teach, great combination of tactics and strategies, gorgeous to look at.  

With three expansion modules inside the base game, it entices new gamers with immersive play while satisfying the veteran euro-crowd.  Love it.  

The game board is beautiful.  Part of the enjoyment of playing is contemplating your moves while treating your eyes with the artwork and fresh colors in the different sections of the board.  Best of all is watching the Fresco as it is slowly revealed during gameplay.

One of my daughter's favorite games.  Though she isn't a gamer, she becomes enthralled as she plays this fine title.  Makes gaming dad misty-eyed...  :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
[played using all "advanced" expansions]

The game has quite a clever representational scheme - the correspondence between the primary/secondary/tertiary color mixing levels and the cube sizes.  The size-based system could (and should) be emulated in other cube-trading games.

Also, if you've got a thing for electromagnetic frequencies, the color mixing theme does serve as a helpful mnemonic when determining which pair of "goods" (paints) trades up to another type.

Goods by any other name still bore me to tears, however.  You can't hide a standard cube-swapper under a veneer of artistry and light physics and hope that no one will notice.

In terms of gameplay mishaps, it's despairingly easy to have your face ground into the dirt due to turn order shuffling in the final round.  Apparently, you really, really, really (emphasis on the "really") don't want to be ahead in points compared to your main competitor - or anyone else at all, truthfully - going into the last turn.  That one tile you needed to win?  Oh, it's gone. Sweet.
